 **Vidūshaka— **Hasten her then for the teacher's fee.

 **Māla— **I am so glad you are not proud.

** Bakul.—** I shall feel proud now that I have got feet worthy of my training. \[*Aside*\]. Ah ! My mission is successful. \[*Looking at the painting, aloud*\]. The painting on one of your feet is finished. Only it requires blowing with the mouth. Or rather the place itself is windy.

 **King— **Friend, see—

 Now has arisen a first-rate opportunity for me to serve her by blowing with my mouth her foot on which the lac-paint is still wet. (13)

 **Vidūshaka— **Why this regret ? You will experience likewise for long.

 **Bakul.—** Friend, your foot is blooming like the red lotus May you always roll on the lap of His Majesty?

*\[Irāvati looks at Nipuṇikā's face\]*

***Page 35.***

 **King—** I regard it a blessing for me.

 **Mála.—** Friend, you say the unsayable.

 **Bakul.—** I have said only what is sayable.

 **Māla.—** Indeed I am dear to you.

 **Bakula— **Not only to me.

 **Mala.— **To whom else?

 **Bakul.—** To His Majesty also, who is a lover of good qualities.

** Māla.—** You speak falsely. That is not in me.

 **Bakul.— **True, it is not in you. It is seen in the pallor and emaciation of His Majesty's body.

 **Nipu.—** The reply of the wretch appears to be pre-calculated.

 **Bakul.—** Take for gospel the words of the wise that 'love is the touchstone of love.'

** Mála.— ** Are you speaking of your own accord ?

 **Bakul.—** No, no. These indeed are the words of His Majesty,—sweet with love but in another's mouth.

** Māla.—** Friend, when I think of the queen, my heart does not feel confident.

***Page 36.***

 **Bakul.— **Foolish girl, should a mango-blossom, the all-in-all of spring's advent, not be worn as an ear-ornament, because the bees swarm over it?

 **Māla.—** You will then help me to the utmost in this difficulty.

 **Bakul.—** I am indeed Bakulāvalikā[^1]\* that gives out fragrance when rubbed.

[^1]: # "There is a pun on 'Bakulavalika'—her name—which also means 'a garland of Bakula flowers'."

 ** King—** Bravo, Bakulāvalikā, bravo !

 By conversation which was started after knowing her feelings and in which apt replies were given by you when contradicted, she has been brought round to your instruction. Truly the lives of lovers lie in the hands of the female messengers. (14)

 **Irā.—**See, my girl, Mālavikā has been definitely made to take a step in this affair by Bakulāvalikā.

 **Nipu.—** My lady, a lesson (in love) would excite longing even in a person of steady temperament.

 **Irā.— **Rightly did my heart entertain fears. After having grasped the situation fully, I shall think over it.

 **Māla.—** Here I have finished the painting of your second foot also. I shall just put the anklets on both. \[*Gesticulates Putting on the pair of anklets*\] Friend, get up and execute the queen's command which would make the Aśoka blossom.

*\[Both get up\]*

 **Irā.—** Did you hear that it was the queen's command ? Let it be so.

 **Bakul.— **Intensely flaming and fit for enjoyment, here is before you—

 **Māla.— **\[*Joyfully*\] His lordship?

**Page 37.**

 **Bakul.— **No, not his lordship—but this bunch of tender leaves hanging from the branch of the Aśoka, Make it your ear-ornament.

 **Vidūshaka— **Did your honour hear?

 **King—** Friend, this is sufficient for lovers. I have no love for a union, even if accomplished, between persons one of whom is indifferent and the other full of longing. Preferable to it is even the death of lovers who are hopeless of meeting each other but reciprocate each other's love. (15)

*\[After making an ear-ornament of the sprout, Mālavikā playfully strikes the Aśoka with her foot.\]*

 **King—** Friend,

 After taking the sprout for her ear-ornament from this tree, she has given her foot in return. By this suitable exchange between the two I consider myself to be cheated. (16)

 **Māla.—** Is it likely that our expectations would come true?

 **Bakul.—** Friend, it will be no fault of yours. This Aśoka will be worthless if, even after receiving such an honour from your foot, it is slow in putting forth flowers.

 **King—** If, O Aśoka, after being honoured by the slender-waisted lady with this foot which is resounding with the jingling anklet and is as tender as a fresh lotus, you do not put forth flowers at once, in vain do you cherish the longing common to all gay lovers. (17)

 Friend, I wish to enter by first creating an opportunity for speaking.

***Page 38.***

 **Vidūshaka—** Come, I shall crack a joke with her.

*\[Both enter\]*

 **Nipu.—** My lady, here is his lordship entering.

** Irā.—** My heart had already anticipated this.

 **Vidūshaka—** \[*Approaching*\] Madam, is it proper for you to strike with your left foot this Aśoka, the favourite of his honour?

 **Both—** \[*In confusion*\] Oh, his lordship !

 **Vidūshaka—** Bakulāvalikā, when you knew the fact why didn't you prevent her ladyship from doing this improper act ?

*\[Mālavikā shows fear\]*

 ** Nipu.—** See, my lady, what the noble Gautama has started.

 **Irā.— **How else would this wretch of a Brāhmana find a living?

 **Baku.—** Sir, she is executing the queen's orders. She is therefore not responsible for this transgression. Be gracious, my lord *\[Saying so, she along with herself makes Mālavikā prostrate\]*

 **King—** If it is so, you are not guilty, Get up, my good lady.

 *\[With these words he takes her by the hand and raises her.\]*

 **Vidūshaka— **Right you are. In this matter the queen should be respected.

***Page 39.***

 **King— **O charming lady of beautiful thighs, I hope your left foot, tender as a sprout, which hit the hard trunk of the tree, is not hurt. (18)

*\[M*ā*lavik*ā* blushes\]*

 **Irá.— **(*Jealously*) Oh, my lord has a heart as soft as fresh butter.

 **Malā.— **Come, Bakulāvalikā, let us inform the queen that the duty assigned to us has been performed.

 **Baku.—**Then request his lordship to allow us to go.

 **King—** My good girl, go you will. But first listen to my request for which an occasion has arisen.

** Baku.—** Listen attentively. May your lordship command.

 **King—** For a long time this person has not put forth a flower of satisfaction like that. With your ambrosial touch fulfil also the longing of this person who has no other desire. (19)

 **Irā.—** \[*Suddenly appearing on the scene*\]. Fulfil,—do fulfil. The Aśoka will put forth flowers only. But this person will blossom as well as bear fruit.

*\[All are confused on seeing Irávati.\]*

 **King— ***\[Aside to Vidúshaka\]*. What is the way out?

 **Vidūshaka—** What else? Only the speed of our legs !

***Page 40.***

 **Irā.—** Bakulāvalikā, you made a clever beginning ! Mālavikā, you please fulfil the request of my lord !

 **Both— **My lady, be gracious. Who are we to enjoy the privilege of his lordship's love? \[*Exeunt ambo*\]

 **Irā.—** Oh, how untrustworthy men are ! Quite unsuspecting like a doe attracted by the song of the hunter, I put faith in your words and did not know this.

 **Vidūshaka—** \[*Aside to the King*\]. Invent something now. A burglar, when caught red-handed, has to say, "I am receiving lessons in the art of house-breaking."

 **King—** Fair lady, I had nothing to do with Mālavikā. As you delayed, I somehow diverted myself.

 **Irá.— **You *are* trustworthy ! I never knew that my lord had found such an object for diversion. Otherwise this unfortunate person would not have acted in this way.

** Vidūshaka—** My lady, don't you accuse his lordship of breach of courtesy. If a casual talk with the queen's attendant by chance is made out to be a sin, then you alone are the authority for that.

**Page 41.**

** Irā.— **Well, let it be called 'a casual talk.' Why should I worry myself ? *\[Departs in anger\]*

 **King—** *\[Following her\]* Be gracious, my lady.

 *\[Irāvati, in spite of the girdle entangling her feet, goes on\].*

 **King—** Fair lady, it does not behove you to show indifference to a loving person.

 **Irā.— **Rogue, your heart is not to be trusted !

 **King—** O darling, let your disregard conveyed by the word 'rogue' be shown unto me, who is so familiar with you. But, O angry lady, you do not give up that disregard, even though solicited by your girdle falling at your feet. (20)

 **Irā.—** Even this wretched girdle follows you. *\[Taking up her girdle, she is about to strike the King with it.\]*

 **King—**This**—**

 Angry lady, shedding copious tears, is ready to strike me violently with her golden girdle inadvertently fallen from her hips, like a row of clouds that pouring down a torrent of rain, is ready to smite the Vindhya mountain with a streak of lightning. (21)

 **Irā.—** Why do you make me guilty again in this manner ?

 **King.— **O curly-headed lady, why do you hold back the proposed punishment from a guilty person like me? In this way you enhance your charm and are still angry with your slave. (22)

 Surely this at least is permissible. *\[With these words he falls at her feet.\]*

***Page 42.***

 **Irā.—** These are not the feet of Mālavikā, which would fulfil your desire for a thrill. *\[Exit with her maid-servant\]*

 ** Vidūshaka— **Get up. You have indeed been favoured.

 **King— ***\[Getting up, not seeing Irávatí\]* What ! Is my beloved gone?

 **Vidūshaka— **Friend, it is fortunate that she has gone unreconciled owing to your immodest conduct. Let us then go away soon, lest she should return like the planet Mars retrograding to the zodiacal sign.

 ** King—** Oh, the perversity of love !

 With my mind drawn towards my sweetheart, I consider her disregard of my prostration as an act of service. For, only thus it is possible for me to neglect that loving lady in her anger. (23)

*\[Exit with his friend\]*

*End of Act III*

**——————**

**ACT IV**

***Page 43.***

*\[Enter the love-lorn King and a female door-keeper\]*

 **King—** *\[Aside\]* May the tree of love, which took root with my desire for her when her name reached my ears, which put forth sprouts of passion when she crossed my vision, and which shot out buds, as it were, when the touch of her hand caused my hair to bristle visibly—make me, who is languishing, know the taste of its fruit. (1)

 *\[Aloud\]* O friend Gautama !

 **Door-keeper— **May your lordship be victorious ! Gautama is away.

 **King—** *\[Aside\]* Yes. He has been sent by me to get news about Mālavikā.

*\[Entering\]*

 **Vidūshaka—** May your honour prosper.

 **King—** O Jayasenā, just find out where queen Dhāriṇī is and how she is being amused, as her foot is aching.

** Door-keeper— **As Your Majesty commands. *\[Exit\]*

 **King—** Friend, what is the news about her ladyship, your friend?

 **Vidūshaka—**The same as of a cuckoo in the clutches of a cat.

 **King— **\[*Sorrowfully\]* How so?

***Page 44***

 **Vidūshaka—** The poor soul has been thrown by the tawny-eyed lady into an underground cell for valuables, as if into the jaws of death

 **King— **On coming to know of my meeting with her, I suppose ?

 **Vidūshaka— **Yes.

 **King— **Who could be so hostile to me as to incense the queen against me?

 **Vidūshaka— **Please listen. Parivrājikā has told me -'Yesterday her ladyship Irāvatī came to inquire about the welfare of the queen whose foot is aching?

 ** King— **Go on.

 **Vidūshaka― **Then she was asked by the queen if 'the favoured person' was visited by His Majesty. She replied**—**'Futile is your compliment, since you know that the favouriteship has now passed to your attendant.'

 **King— **Even without explicit mention the statement makes one's suspicions fall on Mālavikā.

 ** Vidūshaka—**Then pressed by her, she apprised the queen of your misbehaviour.

 **King— **Oh, how persistent is the anger of her ladyship ! Well, what further?

 **Vidūshaka— **What else but that both Mālavikā and Bakulāvalikā, their feet in chains, are now enjoying a residence in the nether world never visited by the sunlight, like two Nága maidens.

 **King—** Alas, alas !

***Page 45.***

 The sweet-voiced cuckoo and the bee while hovering about the blooming mango tree have been driven into its hollow by an unseasonable shower preceded by a strong wind. (2)

 Is there any scope for some expedient here?

 **Vidūshaka— **How can there be? For, Mādhavikā, who has been posted at the treasure-house, has been ordered by the queen-—“Unless you see the seal of my signet-ring, you are not to release the wicked Mālavikā and Bakulāvalikā."

 **King— ***\[Heaving a sigh, and striking him gently\]* Friend,what is to be done now?

 **Vidūshaka― ***\[Reflecting\]* There is an expedient.

 **King—** What's that?

 **Vidūshaka—** *\[Glancing around\]* Some one may hear unseen. I shall whisper in your ear. *\[Drawing near, in his ear\]* Like this. *\[Reveals\]*

 **King—** *\[Joyfully\]* Well thought. Employ it for success.

*\[Entering\]*

 ** Door-keeper— **Your Majesty, the queen is lying in the airy bedroom with her foot besmeared with red sandal and held in the hands of her servants, and is being amused by the holy lady with many a story.

 **King— **Then this is an excellent occasion for my entry.

 **Vidūshaka— **Your honour may therefore go. I shall also take some sort of present in my hand, as I have to visit the queen.

 **King— **Go, but first take Jayasenā into confidence.

***Page 46.***

 **Vidūshaka— **Very well *\[In her ear\]* O lady, like this *\[Revealing, exit\]*

** Vidūshaka— **There, as I stretched out my hand to pluck a bunch of Aśoka flowers, I was bitten by death itself in the form of a snake that had come out of a hollow. See, here are the two bite-marks.

 **Parivrājikā― **It is said that the first thing to do is to cut off the bitten part. Let that be done in his case.

 Cutting off or cauterizing of the bitten part or letting out of blood from the wound**—**these remedies if employed just after persons are bitten, may save their lives. (4)

 **King—** Now this is a job for a poison-doctor, O Jayasenā, bring Dhruvasiddhi at once.

 ** Door-keeper— **As your Majesty commands. *\[Exit\]*

 **Vidūshaka— **Oh, I am seized by this vile death.

 ** King— **Don't be alarmed. The bite may perhaps be non-poisonous.

 **Vidūshaka—** Why shall I be not alarmed? My limbs are simmering. *\[Indicates the effect of poison\]*

***Page 48.***

 ** Queen—** Ah, his contortions forebode a calamity. Hold the Brāhmana.

*\[The attendants hold him hurriedly.\]*

** Vidūshaka—** Oh, I have been your dear friend from child-hood. Bearing this in mind, you will look after the welfare of my mother who will be childless.

 **King—** Don't be afraid, compose yourself. Ere long the doctor will cure you.

*\[Entering\]*

 **Jayasenā— **Your Majesty, Dhruvasiddhi, being ordered to come, begs to say, "Let Gautama be brought here."

 **King—** Then take him to the doctor carried by eunuchs.

 ** Jayasenā— **Very well.

** Vidūshaka—** *\[Looking at the queen\]* Madam, I may or may not survive. Please excuse whatever wrong I might have done you while serving his honour.

 ** Queen—** May you live long !

*\[Exeunt Vidūshaka and the female door-keeper\]*

 **King— **This poor fellow is, by nature, a coward. He has no confidence in the craft of even Dhruvasiddhi, appropriately so called.

*\[Entering\]*

 **Jayasentā— **May Your Lordship be victorious. Dhruvasiddhi requests—'Something bearing on it the mark of a snake must be furnished in connection with the 'Udakumbha' magicrite. So let that be found.'

***Page 49.***

 **Queen— **There is a ring bearing the mark of a snake. Please give it into my hand afterwards.

*\[Taking it, the female door-keeper starts\]*

 **King—** Jayasenā, bring me the news at once in the event of success.

 **Door-keeper—** As Your Majesty commands.

 ** Parivrājikā— **As my heart speaks, Gautama is free from the effects of poison.

 **King— **May it be so.

*\[Entering\]*

 **Jayasenā— **Victory to Your Majesty. The effect of poison being removed, Gautama came back to himself in a moment.

 **Queen— **Happily I am freed from blame.

 **Door-keeper— **The minister Vāhatava requests—'Many state affairs are to be discussed. I, therefore, want the favour of an audience.'

 ** Queen— **May my lord go to attend to his duties.  
————————————————————————————  
\* lit. 'Infallible-cure.' There is a pun on the word.

———————————————————————————————

 **King― **This place is now exposed to the sun. And cooling treatment is recommended for this pain. Let the couch, therefore, be removed to another place.

 **Queen―** Girls, carry out the orders of my lord.

 **Attendants― **Very well.

*\[Exeunt the queen, Parivrájiká and the attendants\]*

 **King―** Jayasenā take me to the pleasure-grove by the secret passage.

***Page 50.***

 ** Door-keeper―** This way my lord, this way.

 **King— **Jayasenā, I suppose, Gautama has finished his job.

 **Door-keeper― **Yes.

 **King― **Even though the plan for attaining the cherished object is singularly effective, my timid mind, believing it to be dubious, is sceptic of success. (5)

*\[Entering\]*

 **Vidūshaka— **May your honour prosper. Your happy affairs have been accomplished.

 ** King―** Jayasenā, you too attend to your duty.

 **Door-keeper―** As Your Majesty commands. *\[Exit\]*

 **King―** Friend, Mādhavikā must be a simpleton. Did she not hesitate in the least?

 **Vidūshaka― **Having seen the queen's signet-ring she could hardly hesitate.

 **King― **I am not referring to the signet-ring at all. She ought to have asked such like questions—"Why are these two prisoners being released? Why have you been chosen to carry this message in preference to the queen's attendants?"

 **Vidūshaka― **Certainly she did. But notwithstanding my having a dull head, I proved quite ready-witted at the time.

 **King―** Tell me, how ?

** Vidūshaka— **I said to her, "The king has been told by the fortune-tellers: "Your stars are portentous. Let all the prisoners be set free."

 **King— ***\[Joyfully\]* Then—

***Page 51.***

 **Vidūshaka—** On hearing this the queen out of her regard for Irāvatī's feelings sent me with this message, making it appear as if the king was releasing all prisoners. 'That is right,' saying so, she did the job.

 **King— ***\[Embracing Vidūshaka\]* Friend, dear am I to you.

 Not only by virtue of intelligence friends can see the object in view— the subtle path leading to the realization of a thing can be discerned by love also. (6)

 ** Vidūshaka—** Please make haste. Having left Mālavikā with her friend in the Summer-house, I have come to meet you.

 **King— **I am to greet her. Lead me.

 **Vidūshaka— **Come, your honour. *\[Waking about\]* Here is the Summer-house.

 ** King— ***\[Apprehensively\]* Friend, here is Chandrikā, the attendant of your friend Irāvati, drawing nigh while busy collecting flowers. Therefore let us conceal ourselves behind the wall.

 **Vidūshaka—** Oh, Chandrikā[^2] must be avoided by thieves and lovers.

[^2]: # "*—Lit moonlight. There is a pun on the word."

*\[Both do as stated\]*

 **King— **Gautama, how might your friend be waiting for me? Come, let us look at her from this window.

 **Vidūshaka— **All right.

*\[Both stand looking at her\]*

*\[Enter Málaviká and Bakulâvalikȧ\]*



***Page 52.***

 **Bakulāvalikā— **Friend, salute his lordship.

 **King—** Methinks she is showing my picture.

 **Mālavikā— ***\[Joyfully\]* I salute you. *\[Looking at the door, sorrowfully\]* Friend, you are deceiving me.

 **King—** Friend, I am am delighted at the joy and sorrow of her ladyship.

 The face of the lovely lady has at once assumed both the conditions of the white lotus—the one which it presents at sunrise and the other which it exhibits at sunset. (7)

 **Bakulāvalikā— **Why, Here is his lordship in a picture !

 **Both—** *\[Bowing low\]* Victory to his lordship.

 **Mālavikā― **Friend, the other day I was not so gratified with seeing the beauty of my lord when I stood face to face with him, as I am to-day when I am looking at him in the picture.

 ** Vidūshaka— **Did you hear? Her ladyship says—"You did not look so well, as you do in the picture. In vain do you take pride in your youth, like a chest containing jewels.

 **King—** Friend, Women, though full of curiosity, are bashful by nature.

 They long to have a full view of the handsome figure of their lovers when they meet them for the first time, and yet the glances of these large-eyed ladies do not fall fully on them. (8)

***Page 53.***

 ** Mālavikā—** Friend, who is this lady that is being gazed at with an affectionate glance by his lordship with his face turned sideways ?

 **Bakulāvalikā—** Why, She is Iravāti by his side.

 **Mālavikā— **Friend, to me the king appears to be somewhat impolite in that, ignoring all the other queens, he has fixed his gaze on the face of one alone.

 **Bakulāvalikā― ***\[Aside\]* Taking the king in the picture to be real, she is getting jealous ! Never mind. I shall have some fun with her. *\[Aloud\]* Friend, she is the king's favourite.

** Mālavikā― **Then why shall I worry myself? *\[Turns away in jealousy\]*

 **King―** Friend, see―

 By turning away in jealousy her face with the lower lip quivering and the mark on the forehead broken by her frown, she has, as it were, given an exhibition of her teacher's instruction in graceful acting at the time of showing anger towards an erring lover. (9)

 **Vidūshaka― **Now be ready to conciliate her.

 ** Mālavikā―** The noble Gautama also is assisting him here. *\[Again wants to turn about\]*

***Page 54.***

 **Bakulávaliká— ***\[Preventing Mālavikā\]* Surely you are not angry now?

 **Mālavikā— **If you consider me to be angry for long, I just recall my anger.

 ** King— ***\[Approaching\]*

 O lotus-eyed lady, why are you angry with me for my conduct shown in the picture: Here am I in person, your exclusive slave. (10)

 **Bakulāvalikā— **Victory to your lordship.

 **Mālavikā— ***\[Aside\]* Strange indeed that I got angry with the king in the picture. *\[With a bashful look on her face she folds her hands\]*

*(The king shows nervousness of love)*

 **Vidūshaka— **Why do you look indifferent ?

** King― **Owing to the untrustworthiness of your friend.

 **Vidūshakā― **Such a lack of trust in her ladyship !

 **King―** Listen—

 Standing within the range of my sight in the dream, she vanishes in a trice. Coming within the clasp of my arms, your friend slips away instantly. Thus, afflicted as I am withn the agony of love by this phantoṃ of union, how can my mind, O friend, have any confidence in her ? (11)

***Page 55***

 **Bakulāvalikā― **Friend, his lordship has been duped many a time. Now make yourself worthy of his confidence.

 **Mālavikā—** But, friend, to an unfortunate person like me a union with his lordship is denied even in a dream.

 ** Bakulāvalikā— **What has his lordship to say to this ?

 **King—** 'Tis idle to make a reply. I offer my own self to your friend in the presence of the fire of Cupid. She is not to serve me. I am to serve her in private. (12)

 **Vidūshaka—** *\[Walking about, excitedly\]* O Bakulāvalikā, here is a deer damaging the leaves of the young Aśoka. Come, let us drive it away.

 **Bakulāvalikā— **Very well *\[Starts\]*

 ** King—** Friend, in the same way you ought to be all careful at this moment when we need protection.

 **Vidūshaka— **Need Gautama be instructed even to this effect ?

 **Bakulāvalikā― ***\[Walking about\]* Noble Gautama, I shall keep dark**—**You guard the door-way.

 **Vidūshaka— **Right.

*\[Exit Bakulāvalikā\]*

 **Vidūshaka—** I shall seat myself on this crystal pavement. *\[Doing so\]* Oh, how pleasant is the touch of this excellent stone ! *\[Goes off to sleep\]*

***Page 56***

*\[Mālavikā stands full of fear\]*

 ** King—** O fair lady, give up the fear of union with me who has been long since yearning for you. Adopt the conduct of the Atimukta creeper towards me who has assumed the role of a mango tree.

 ** Mālavikā― **For fear of the queen I can't do what is dear to me.

 ** King―** Oh, fear not.

 **Mālavikā— ***\[Tauntingly\]* In the presence of her ladyship I witnessed the courage of my lord who fears not.

 **King―** Politeness, O Bimba-lipped one, is the family vow of the descendants of Bimbaka; nevertheless; O large-eyed lady, what life I have depends entirely on the hope of obtaining you. (14)

 Therefore, favour this person now who has long been in love with you. *\[Gesticulates embracing\]*

*\[Mālavikā gesticulates avoiding\]*

** King— ***\[Aside\]* Charming indeed are the actions of young ladies in love-sport. For—

 All a-quiver, she stops my hand as my fingers fumble with her girdle. She screens her breasts with her hands as I clasp her for a close embrace. She turns away her face with long eye-lashes as I raise it for a kiss. Even by a show of resistance she assuredly brings me the pleasure of the fulfilment of my desires. (15)

*\[Enter Irāvatī and Nipuṇika\]*

***Page 57.***

 **Irāvatī― **O Nipuṇikā, my girl, did Chandrikā really tell you that the noble Gautama was seen sleeping alone on the summer-house terrace?

 **Nipuṇikā—** Otherwise how could I dare tell your ladyship?

 ** Irāvatī— **Then let us go there to inquire after my lord's dear friend lately saved from danger and...

 **Nipuṇikā—** My lady, your sentence seems to be incomplete.

 **Irāvatī—** And to propitiate my lord in the picture.

 ** Nipuṇikā—** Why not propitiate the king himself ?

 **Irāvatī—** O silly girl, my lord whose heart is now transferred to another is for me as good as drawn in a picture. This attempt is only to make amends for my incivility.

 **Nipuṇikā— **This way, my lady, this way.

*\[Both walk about\]*

*\[Entering\]*

 **Maid-Servant— **Victory to your ladyship. The queen says, "It is no time for me to be jealous. But just to add to the great regard I have for you, Mālavikā has been put in fetters along with her friend. If you approve that I do a good turn to my lord, I shall do so. Please tell me what you desire."

***Page 58.***

 **Irāvatī—** O Nagarikā, inform the queen—Who am I to direct her ladyship? She has done me a favour by putting the attendants in fetters. By whose grace do I prosper, if not hers?

** Maid-Servant— **Very well. *\[Exit\]*

 **Nipuṇikā— ***\[Walking about and seeing\]* My lady, here at the door of the summer-house has the noble Gautama gone off to sleep while sitting, like a bull in the market-place.

 **Irāvatī— **O dear me ! I hope the evil effect of poison is not still lingering.

** Nipuṇikā― **His face wears a pleasing look. Besides, he has been treated by Dhruvasiddhi. Therefore nothing untoward should be apprehended in his case.

 **Vidūshaka— ***\[Talks in sleep\]* O lady Mālavikā.

 **Nipuṇikā—** Has my lady heard? To whom can this wretched rogue ever be a friend[^3]? Having all these days filled his stomach with our sweetmeats offered to him on the occasion of the invocation of blessings, he now talks of Mālavikā in his sleep.

[^3]: # "There may be a pun here. The original Sanskrit word for 'friend' is ' आत्मनीनः' which also means 'the jester in a play,'"

***Page 59.***

 **Vidūshaka— **May you surpass Irāvatī?

 **Nipuṇikā—** This is too much. From behind the pillar, with this wooden stick crooked as a snake I shall frighten this wretch of a Brahmaṇa who is afraid of serpants.

 **Irāvatī— **This ungrateful fellow certainly deserves this punishment.

*\[Nipuṇikā throws the wooden stick at Vidūshaka\]*

 **Vidūshaka—** *\[Walking up with a start\]* Help, help ! A snake has fallen on me.

 ** King—** *\[Approaching in all haste\]* Friend, fear not, fear not.

 ** Mālavikā— ***\[Following\]* My lord, pray don't go out in haste. 'A snake,' that is what he says.

 **Irāvatī—** O dear me ! My lord is running in this very direction.

 **Vidūshaka—** *\[With a laugh\]* What, a wooden stick ! I however thought that I had got the reward of my making a wound with the Ketaki thorns and passing it off as a snake-bite.

\[Entering with a toss of the curtain\]

** Bakulāvalikā— **Don't you enter, my lord. Here is seen something looking like a crawling snake.

 **Irāvatī― ***\[Approaching the king from behind the pillar\]* I hope this day–appointment of the couple proved fruitful without any hitch.

***Page 60.***

*\[All are upset on seeing Irāvatī\]*

 **King—** O dear, 'tis a novel greeting.

 **Irāvatī― **Bakulāvalikā, fortunately your vow with regard to your role as a go–between has been redeemed.

 **Bakulāvalikā— **May my lady be gracious. Does the god of rain forget the earth, simply because the frogs croak?

 **Vidūshaka— **Oh, no. At the mere sight of your ladyship, his lordship has forgotten your disregard of his prostration. But your ladyship does not relent even now.

 ** Irāvatī— **Even if I am angry, what can I do?

 **King— **It does not behove you to be angry without cause.

For―

 O fair lady, has your face ever worn an expression of anger without cause even for a moment ? Say how can the night have the orb of the moon eclipsed by the planet Rahu, except on a full-moon day. (16)

 **Irāvatī―** 'Without cause'—finely said indeed, my lord ! If I show anger at my good fortune passing on to another, I only expose myself to ridicule.

 **King— **You misinterpret it. But I, as a matter of fact, see no ground for your anger. For—

***Page 61.***

 In spite of an offence, attendants ought not be kept in bondage on festal days. For this reason these two were released by me, and they have come to pay their homage to me. (17)

 **Irāvatī― **O Nipuṇikā, go and tell the queen, "To-day indeed I have seen your partiality for me !"

 **Nipuṇikā―** All right. *\[Exit\]*

 **Vidūshaka―** Oh, what a mishap ! The domesticated pigeon escaping from confinement has fallen a prey to a kite.

*\[Entering\]*

 ** Nipuṇikā―** *\[Aside to Irāvatī\]* My lady, Mādhavikā whom I met by chance told me that it happened like this *\[Whispers in her car\]*

 **Irāvatī― ***\[Aside\]* I see. Truly, this plan must have been devised by this wretch of a Brahmaṇa.

* \[Looking at Vidūshaka, aloud\]* This is the policy of this minister of love-affairs.

 **Vidūshaka―** Madam, if I ever read even a single syllable of policy, may I forget even the Gāyatri.

 **King―** \[Aside) How shall I extricate myself from this difficulty ?

*\[Entering\]*

***Page 62.***

 **Jayasenā— ***\[In agitation\]* Your Majesty, the princess Vasulakshmi, while running after a ball, was greatly frightened by the tawny monkey. Sitting on the queen's lap and trembling like a tender leaf in the wind, she does not recover herself at all.

 **King— **Alas ! Children are naturally timid.

 **Irāvatī— **My lord, go in haste to comfort her lest her condition caused by the fright should grow worse.

 **King— **I shall presently bring her round. *\[Goes off in haste\]*

 **Vidūshaka— ***\[Aside\]* Well done, O tawny monkey. You have well saved your friend from the difficulty.

*\[Exeunt the King with his friend, Irāvatī, Nipuṇikā and the female door-keeper\]*

 ** Mālavikā— **Friend, as I think of the queen my heart trembles, I know not what I shall have to experience next.

*\[Voice behind the scenes\]*

 How wonderful ! Even before five nights had expired since the fulfilment of its longing, the golden Aśoka is laden with buds. I shall just inform the queen.

***Page 63.***

*\[Both are delighted to hear it\]*

 **Bakulāvalikā— **Cheer up, my friend. The queen is ever true to her word.

 **Mālavikā―**Then let us follow the keeper of the pleasure garden.

 **Bakulāvalikā― **Very well.

*\[Exeunt ambo\]*

End of Act IV.

**ACT V**

***Page 64.***

*\[Enter the female Garden-keeper\]*

 **Garden-keeper— **I have finished the construction of the dais round the golden Aśoka which has been duly honoured. I shall now inform the queen that I have executed her orders *\[Walking about\]* Oh ! The gods seem to be gracious towards Mālavikā. The queen, who was so wroth with her, will now be favourably disposed towards her on hearing the news of the golden Aśoka. Where would the queen be? *\[Looking round\]* Oh, here is the hunchback, Sárasaka, the confidant attendant of the queen, coming out of the quandrangle and carrying with him a box sealed with lac. *\[Enter the hunckback as stated\]*

 **Garden-keeper— ***\[Approaching\]* Sārasaka, where are you going?

 **Sārasaka—** Madhukarikā, the daily fee has to be given to the learned Brāhmaṇas. I am going to deliver it into the hands of the noble priest[^4].

[^4]: # "पुरोहित—a family priest, a domestic chaplain."

** Garden-keeper— **What for ?

***Page 65.***

 ** Sārasaka—** Ever since it was heard that the Senāpaṭi had appointed prince Vasumitra as the guard of the sacrificial horse, the queen gives away in charity eighteen golden coins to the deserving for the safety of the prince's life.

 **Garden-keeper— **Right. But where is the queen and what is she doing ?

 **Sárasaka— **Sitting on a seat in the 'auspicious mansion', she is hearing from her clerks the letter sent by her brother Vīrasena from Vidarbha.

 **Garden-keeper— **But what is the news about the king of Vidarbha ?

 **Sárasaka— **It is reported that the king of Vidarbha has been subjugated by the victorious forces of our king under the command of Vīrasena and his cousin Mādhavasena has been set free. Further, he has sent an envoy to our king with a present of costly jewels, vehicles, and a body of attendants mostly women artists. The envoy will interview His Majesty to-morrow.

 **Garden-keeper— **Go, do your work. I too shall see the queen.

***\[Exeunt ambo\]***

**End of the Interlude**

*\[Enter the Female Door-keeper\]*

 ** Door-keeper— **I have been ordered by the queen who is engrossed in doing honour to the Aśoka—'Request my lord that I wish to see the splendour of the blossoming Aśoka in his company.' I shall in the meantime wait for His Majesty who is now holding his court. *\[Walks about\]*

***Page 66.***

*\[Behind the scenes, two bards\]*

 **First—** Fortunately Your Majesty has vanquished your foes by means of your forces.

 You, delighting in the sweet songs of the cuckoos, spend the springtide in the gardens on the banks of the Vidiśā like Cupid himself in the company of Rati; while your enemy, O giver of boons, lies prostrate along with the trees on the banks of the Varadā, which served as posts to tie the victorious elephants belonging to you who are so mighty. (1)

 **Second— **O God-like king, the exploits of both you and Kṛṣṇa with regard to the Vidarbhas[^5] have been celebrated in verse by the poets out of hero-worship—of you who has snatched perforce the glory of the Vidarbha by means of his forces, and Kṛṣṇa who forcibly carried off Rukmiṇī by the might of his arms as massive as the iron-bars of a gate. (2)

[^5]: # "क्रथकैशिकाः is another name of Vidarbhas,"

 **Door-keeper— **Oh, his lordship is coming in this very direction, his departure being announced by shouts of victory. I also, moving a little out of his way, shall resort to the arch of this front terrace. *\[Stands aside\]*

*\[Enter the king with his friend\]*

  **King— **Thinking on the one hand that the union with my beloved is far from easy and hearing on the other that the king of Vidarbha has been subdued by my forces, my heart grieves as well as rejoices like the lotus beaten by showers in sunshine. (3)

***Page 67.***

** Vidūshaka—** So far as I see, your honour will be thoroughly happy.

 **King— **How?

 **Vidūshaka— **To-day, I understand, the queen said to the learned Kauśikī, "Revered lady, since you pride yourself on your art of decoration, why not robe Mālavikā in a wedding-dress of the Vidarbha style." And accordingly Mālavikā has been adorned by her exquisitely. Her Majesty may perhaps fulfil your desire also.

 **King— **Friend, it is quite possible, judging from the previous conduct of Dhāriṇī who has lost all sense of jealousy out of her regard for me.

 ** Door-keeper— ***\[Approaching\]* Victory to Your Majesty. The queen begs to say, "Please make my toil fruitful by witnessing the splendour of the blossoming of the golden Aśoka."

 **King—** Is the queen there?

 **Door-keeper— **Why, yes. Leaving the ladies of the seraglio, who were pleased at being duly honoured, she is awaiting Your Majesty with her attendants led by Mālavikā.

 **King—** *\[Looking joyfully at Vidūshaka\]* Jayasenā, lead the way.

 **Door-keeper— **This way, Your Majesty, this way. *\[All walk about\].*

 **Vidūshaka— ***\[Observing\]* O friend, in the pleasure-garden the spring appears to be somewhat past its youth.

 **King— **You are right.

***Page 68.***

 The waning youth of the season when the Kurbaka flowers lie scattered everywhere and the mango trees are breaking under the weight of their fruit, makes the mind wistful. (4)

** Vidūshaka—** *\[Walking about\]* Hallo, here is the golden Aṣoka which is wearing a mantle of flowers as it were. Please look at it.

 **King—** Rightly was it slow in putting forth flowers. For, now it wears an extraordinary splendour. See—the flowers of all the Aśoka trees that were first in displaying the wealth of springs are transferred, as it were, to this Aśoka, of which the longing has been satisfied. (5)

 **Vidūshaka— **Oh, be confident. Even though we are near, Dhāriṇi allows Mālavikā to remain by her side.

 ** King—** *\[Joyfully\]* Friend, see—

 Here is the queen coming forward to greet me with my beloved respectfully following her, like the Earth herself with the goddess of Royalty who has forgotten to take the lotus in her hand. (6)

*\[Enter Dhariṇī, Parivrájiká and Málaviká and the attendants in order of rank\]*

 ** Mālavikā— *\[****Aside\]* I know the reason why I am robed in wedding attire. Nevertheless, my heart trembles like water on a lotus-leaf. But at the same time my left eye throbs again and again.

 **Vidūshaka—** O** **friend, her ladyship Mālavikā appears exquisitely beautiful in a wedding dress.

***Page 69.***

 **King—** I see her—who,

 Clad in a siiken robe not hanging very loosely and decked with a number of ornaments, appears to me like a night in the month of Chaitra with stars in the mistless sky[^6] and the moon about to rise. (7)

[^6]: # "Lit. with the stars free from mist."

 **Queen— ***\[Approaching\]* Victory to my lord.

** Vidūshaka—** May your ladyship prosper.

 **Parivrājikā― **Victory to Your Majesty.

 **King―** Revered lady, I salute you.

 **Parivrājikā—** May you gain the desired object.

 ** Queen—** *\[With a smile\]* My lord, this Aśoka tree has been fixed as a rendezvous for you and the young ladies.

 **Vidūshaka—** Oh, you are favoured.

 **King— ***\[Blushes and walks round the Aśoka\].*

 This Aśoka is not at all undeserving of such honours by your ladyship, since in contempt of the command of the goddess of Spring it has shown its regard for your effort by blossoming. (8)

 **Vidūshaka—** O friend, without the least fear look at this, that is in full bloom.

 ** Queen—** At whom?

 **Vidūshaka―** Madam, at the floral beauty of the golden Aśoka.

*\[All sit down\]*

 ** King—** *\[Looking at Mālavikā, aside\]* Painful indeed is separation even in close proximity.

 I am the Chakravāka bird, and my beloved is, as it were, its mate. Dhāriṇī, not permitting our union, is like the night. (9)

*\[Entering\]*

***Page 70.***

 **Chamberlain― **Victory to Your Majesty. Sire, the minister begs to say: “Among the presents from the kingdom of Vidarbha, there were two women artists who were not presented to Your Majesty, as they were exhausted by the fatigue of the journey. Now that they are fit to appear before you, Your Majesty may be pleased to give orders."

 **King— **Usher them in.

** Chamberlain—** As Your Majesty commands. *\[Going out, and re-entering with them\]* This way, ladies, this way.

 **First—** *\[Aside, to the other\]* Friend Madanikā, my heart feels cheerful as I enter the wonderful royal palace.

 **Second— **Jyotsnikā, so does mine. There is indeed a popular saying that the state of one's heart augurs the coming joy or sorrow.

 **First—** May that be true now.

 **Chamberlain—** Here is His Majesty with the queen. Ladies. you may approach.

*\[Both advance\]*

*\[Mālavikā and Parivrājikā exchange glances on seeing the two maids\]*

** Both— ***\[Bowing low\]* Victory to the king. Victory to the queen.

 **King—** In what art are you skilled?

 **Both—** Your lordship, we are conversant with music.

 **King—** Queen, take one of these two.

 ** Queen— **Mālavikā, look here. Which of the two would you like to have as your companion in music?

** Both—** *\[Looking at Mālavikā\].* Oh, the princess ! *\[Bowing\]* Victory, victory to the princess. *\[Both of them shed tears along with Mālavikā\].*

*\[All stand looking in wonder\]*

***Page 71.***

** King— **Who are you? Who is she?

 **Both—** Your Majesty, she is our princess.

 **King—** How?

 ** Both—** Your lordship may please listen. She is Mālavikā, the younger sister of prince Mádhavasena whose release was brought about by your lordship after subduing the king of Vidarbha.

** Queen— **What! Is she a princess? I have indeed profaned the sandal-wood by using it for sandals.

 **King—** How was her ladyship reduced to this plight?

 **Mālavikā— ***\[Sighing, aside\]* By the decree of fate.

 **Second.—** Your lordship may please listen. When our prince, Mādhavasena, fell into the hands of his kinsman, his minister, the noble Sumati, secretly took her away, leaving behind the attendants like us.

 **King—** This much I have already heard. What next?

 **Second— **Your lordship, we know nothing beyond this.

 **Parivrājikā—** I, the ill-starred one, shall relate further.

 ** Both—** Princess, her voice is like that of the noble Kauśikī.

***Page 72.***

 **Mālavikā—** Why, it is she herself.

 **Both—** The noble Kauśikī is difficult to recognize in the garb of an ascetic. Revered lady, we salute you.

 **Parivrājikā― **May you fare well.

 **King— **What ! Revered lady, is this the circle of your friends?

 **Parivrājikā— **Yes.

 **Vidūshaka— **Revered Madam, then please relate to us the rest of her ladyship's story.

 **Parivrājikā— ***\[Painfully\]* Then listen. Please know that Sumati, the minister of Mādhavasena, was my elder brother.

 ** King— **I see. What next?

 **Parivrājikā― **Having removed me and the princess whose brother had fared so badly, he joined a caravan bound for Vidiśa with a view to her engagement with you.

 **King—** What next?

 **Parivrājikā―**The caravan after the day's journey, encamped in a forest to rest.

** King—** And then?

 ** Parivrājikā— **Then,

 There appeared a gang of bandits who had quiverbands. tied across their chests, who wore caps of peacock-plumes reaching down to their ears, who had bows in their hands and were shouting, and who were irresistible on account of their sudden onslaught. (10)

*\[Mālavikā gesticulates fear\]*

 **Vidūshaka— **Madam, don't be afraid. The revered lady is relating a past event.

***Page 73.***

 **King—** Then what happened?

 **Parivrājikā—** Then after giving battle for a short while, the escort of the merchants was put to rout by the bandits.

 **King—** Revered lady, the sequel must be painful to hear.

** Parivrājikā― **Then my brother

 Anxious to save at that fateful hour the princess who was afraid of molestation at the hands of the marauders, paid with his dear life the debt to his master whom he loved. (11)

 **First—** Alas, Sumati was killed!

 **Second— **That is why our princess has been reduced to this plight.

*\[Parivrājikā sheds tears\]*

** King—** Revered lady, such is the fate of brave men[^7].The gentleman who showed himself so worthy of his master's salt should not be lamented. What next?

[^7]: # "Lit. mortals,"

 **Parivrājikā― **Then by the time I, who had fainted, came to, the princess was nowhere to be seen.

 **King—** Your revered ladyship has indeed undergone severe tribulations.

** Parivrājikā― **Then having consigned the body of my deceased brother to fire, I, whose grief of widowhood wasn revived, came over to your kingdom and put on the orange-coloured robes of an ascetic.

 **King― **This is the right path for the good. What next?

 **Parivrājikā― **Then the princess, who went from the foresters to Vīrasena and from him to the queen, was again seen by me when I gained admission into the queen's apartments.This is the end of my story.

 ** Mālavikā— ***\[A side\]* I wonder what his lordship would say now.

 ** King― **Oh, calamities bring humiliation in their train. For,

 This lady, fit to bear the title of queen, has been treated as a maid servant, even as one might use a silk garment for a bathing cloth. (12)

***Page 74.***

 **Queen―** Revered lady, it was improper on your part not to have told us that Mālavikā was of noble birth.

 **Parivrājikā― **God forbid! Not so. I had a reason for keeping silent.

 ** Queen―** What's that reason?

 ** King―** If it may be told, please tell it.

 **Parivrājikā—** Please listen. This lady, while her father was alive, was told in my presence by a certain sage whose words were infallible and who had arrived there during his wanderings, that after undergoing servitude for a period of one year she would obtain a husband worthy of her. Hence I consider that I did well in biding my time, seeing that the prophecy about her, which was bound to come true, was being fulfilled by her being in your service.

 **King―** It was but proper that you bided your time.

** Chamberlain— **Sire, I was interrupted by this other story. The minister begs to say, "We have decided as to what is to be done with regard to Vidarbha. I only want to know Your Majesty's wishes."

** King― **Maudagalya, I now wish to establish two kingdoms for the two gentlemen, Yajñasena and Mādhavasena who are cousins.

 Let the two rule separately over the territories on the northern and the southern banks of the Varadā, as the moon and the sun, dividing between themselves, rule over the night and the day. (13)

 **Chamberlain―**Your Majesty, I shall communicate it to the Council of Ministers.

 **King—** *\[Signifies his consent with his finger\]*

 **First—** *\[A side, to Mālavikā\]* Pricess, it is a matter for congratulation that our prince will be established in half of the kingdom.

***Page 75.***

 **Mālavikā―**This in itself is to be considered a great thing that he has been saved from peril of his life.

*\[Entering\]*

 **Chamberlain—** Victory to Your Majesty. The minister, begs to say, "Excellent is Your Majesty's suggestion. The same is the view of the Council of Ministers. For,

 The two kings, bearing the burden of the kingdom equally divided between them and causing no trouble to you on account of mutual restraint will abide by your command, as the two chariot horses, bearing the yoke equally between them and showing no bad temper owing to mutual check, obey the charioteer." (14)

 **King—** Then ask the Council of Ministers to write to Vīrasena, the Commander-in-Chief, to do accordingly.

** Chamberlain—** As Your Majesty commands. *\[Going out and re-entering with a letter accompanied by a present\]* Your Majesty's commands have been executed. But here is a letter along with a present received from His Majesty Pushpamitra, the Senāpati. Your Majesty may be pleased to look through it.

*\[Rising up abruptly, the king takes it with due courtesy and hands over the present to the attendant\]*

*\[And gesticulates the opening of the letter\]*

 ** Queen—** *\[Aside\]* Oh, my heart was already anxious for it. After hearing about welfare of the revered sire, I shall have some news of Vasumitra. Surely the Senapati has appointed my son to a high office.

 **King—** *\[Sits down and reads\]* Blessings. Pushpamitra, the Senāpati, affectionately embraces his son, the long-lived Agnimitra who is at Vidiśa, and informs him from the sacrificial camp.—Be it known to you that the horse which I, who had consecrated myself for the royal sacrifice, let loose to wander about unchecked in the charge of Vasumitra with an escort of a hundred persons and which was to return after a year was seized by a cavalry regiment of the Yavanas while it was wandering on the Southern bank of the Sindhu. Then there ensued a fierce battle between the two armies.

***Page 76.***

*\[The queen exhibits dejection\]*

 **King—** What, it came to such a pass ! *\[Reads further\].* Then Vasumitra, the renowned bowman, having defeated his enemies, got back my noble horse that was being forcibly carried away. (15)

 ** Queen— **This makes me breathe again.

 **King—** *\[Reads the remaining portion of the letter\]* Now that my horse has been brought back by my grandson, I shall complete my sacrifice, as did Sagar whose horse was brought back by Aṃśumat. So, giving up all feeling of anger, you should now come without delay, to attend the sacrifice along with your queens.

 **King—** I esteem it a favour.

 **Parivrājikā— **I congratulate you both on the victory of your son. *\[Looking at the queen\]* By your husband you were placed foremost among the praiseworthy wives of heroes. Now from your son you have got the title 'the mother of a hero.'

 **Vidūshaka―** Madam, I am very glad that the son has taken after his father.

 **Parivrājikā―**The young elephant has but imitated the lordly one.

 **Chamberlain— **My lord, this prince—

 Does not fill our minds with wonder by such a display of valour, inasmuch as an august and irresistible person like yourself is his father as the sage Aurva is of the fire that consumes the water of the ocean. (17)

 **King―** Maudgalya, let all the inmates of the prison, including Yajñasena's brother-in-law, be set at liberty.

 **Chamberlain― **As Your Majesty commands. *\[Exit\]*

 ** Queen―** Jayasenā, go and inform Iravati and other ladies of the harem of the news of the victory of our son.

***Page 77.***

 ** Door-keeper— **Very well. *\[Starts to go\]*

 **Queen―** Just come here.

 **Door-keeper— ***\[Turning back\]* Here am I.

 ** Queen―** *\[Aside, to the door-keeper\]* Having informed Iravatī of the promise I made to Mālavikā when I assigned her the task of fulfilling the longing of the Aśoka and also of her noble birth, plead with her on my behalf, requesting that she should not make me false to my word.

 ** Door-keeper— **As Your Majesty commands. *\[Going out and re-entering\]* My lady, I have been loaded[^8] with the ornaments given to me by the ladies of the harem out of their joy at the victory of your son.

[^8]: #

 ** Queen— **No wonder. This good fortune is as much theirs as mine.

 ** Door-keeper— ***\[Aside, to the queen\]* My lady, Irāvatī further begs to say: "The word already given by the all-powerful queen was right indeed. It is not proper to go back upon it."

 ** Queen— **Revered lady, with your permission I give the hand of Mālavikā to my lord as originally intended by the noble Sumati.

 **Parivājikā— **Even now you have full authority over her.

***Page 78.***

 **Queen— ***\[Holding Mālavikā by the hand\]* My lord, please accept this reward befitting the happy news. *\[The king keeps silent out of shyness\]*

 **Queen― ***\[With a smile\]* What, does my lord slight me?

 **Vidūshaka—** Madam, it is the way of the world that all new bridegrooms are bashful.

 *\[The King looks at Vidūshaka\]*

 ** Vidūshaka— **Or rather, his lordship wishes to accept Mālavikā when she has been favoured with the title of queen by Your Majesty as a special token of love.

 ** Queen—** As she is a princess, the title of queen is already hers by the mere fact of her noble birth. What is the good of repetition?

 **Parivrājikā— **No, not so.

 O blessed lady, precious stones, though dug out of a mine, are not to be set in gold unless they are polished. (18)

 **Queen— ***\[Remembering\]* Excuse me, revered lady. In the talk of the happy event I forgot the proper thing to do. Jayasenā, just go and bring a silk garment.

** Door-keeper—** As Your Majesty commands. *\[Going out and re-entering with the silk garment\]* Your Majesty, here it is.

 ** Queen— ***\[Having veiled Mālavikā with it\]* My lords please accept her now.

***Page 79.***

 **King—** Your ladyship, since it is your command, I dare not go counter to it.

 **Parivrājikā— **Aha ! She has been accepted.

 ** Vidūshaka—** Oh, how well disposed is the queen towards your honour.

*\[The queen looks significantly at the attendants\]*

 **Attendants—** *\[Approaching Mālavikā\]* Victory to the queen.

*\[The queen looks at Parivrájiká\]*

** Parivrājikā― **Considering you, it is nothing strange.

 Noble wives, who love their husbands dearly, serve them even to the extent of admitting a rival; the great rivers bear to the ocean the waters of hundreds of other streams. (19)

*\[Entering\]*

 **Nipuṇikā—** Victory to your lordship. Irāvatī begs to say, "In sinning against my lord by disregarding his courtesy, I in fact acted in a way favourable to him. Now that his desire has been fulfilled, my lord should honour me by only showing his favour.

 ** Queen—** Nipuṇikā, his lordship will certainly accede to her request.

** Nipuṇikā―**Thank you.

 **Parivrājikā—** Your Majesty, if it should so please you, I would like to go and congratulate Mādhavasena who has achieved his object by this matrimonial alliance with you.

 **Queen—** Revered lady, it is not proper for you to leave us.

** King—** Revered lady, in my letters I shall drop a few lines conveying congratulations on your behalf to his honour.

***Page 80.***

 ** Parivrājikā— **Your love leaves me no choice.

 **Queen— **My lord, command me what further service can I render to you.

 ** King—** What further service could there be ? Nevertheless, I wish this—

 O queen, may you always be favourably disposed towards me! this alone you should keep in mind.

*\[Bharatavákya\]*

 As for the wishes of the subjects such as the removal of public calamities, it is not possible that they will not remain fulfilled when Agnimitra is their ruler. (20)

*\[Exeunt omnes\]*

End of Act V of Mālavikāgnimitra written by Kālidāsa.

**APPENDIX I**

**KĀLIDĀSA**

**1. The life of Kālidasā**

 Kālidāsa, the author of Mālavikāgnimitra is beyond dispute the greatest of Indian poets and dramatists. But unfortunately we know next to nothing of his life and age. The poet has observed complete reticence about himself in his works. In the words of Hazlitt "he was the least of an egotist that it was possible to be."

 There are indeed traditional stories about Kālidāsa. But these tales are late, unverifiable and even mutually conflicting and are, therefore, not of much value. 'It almost looks as if—to adopt an idea beautifully described in his poem Kumārasambhava—Time not only destroyed the life but the life-record as well of this Lord of Hearts (Manmatha) and made him Ananga (bodiless) and Kathavaśesha (reduced to a mere name). The life of Kālidāsa cannot be brought by human love or labour into the world of facts and has become a wanderer in Legend-Land.'

**2. The date of Kālidasā**

 The date of Kālidasā is still one of the unsettled problems of Indian Chronology. The dates assigned to him cover the vast period of two thousand years—from the 8th century B.C. to the 12th century A.D.! It is however probable to fix the upper and lower limits of Kālidāsa's date and so to reduce this period.

 The earliest mention of Kālidāsa's name occurs in the Aihole Inscription dated 634 A.D. Bāṇa, who belonged to the first half of the 7th century A.D., pays a high tribute to Kālidāsa in his introduction to Harshacharita. Bhavabhūti, who lived in the 8th century A.D., also refers to Sakuntalā in his Mālatīmādhava. We may, therefore, reject all theories that place Kālidāsa beyond the 7th century A.D. It also follows that the tales connecting him with King Bhoja who ruled at Ujjain in the 11th century A.D. are totally worthless.

 On the other hand Mr.Hippolyte Fauche assigns the 8th century B.C. to the poet. His argument is that Kālidāsa must have lived during the reign of the posthumous child of king Agnivarṇa who is described in the last canto of Raghuvaṃśa. This is a mere fanciful assumption. If the poet was living at that time, he would have described that king also. The fact that no later king of the solar race is described in Raghuvaṃśa does not lead to the conclusion that there were no such later kings. As a matter of reality, the Vishṇu Purāṇa mentions as many as thirty-seven kings who reigned after Agnivarṇa.

 Before assigning a probable date to Kālidāsa we may state that the tradition, in so far as it associates the poet with king Vikramāditya as his patron, is universally admitted. But the question still remains as to who this king was and when he flourished.

 Most of the modern scholars, especially European, place Kālidāsa in the reign of the imperial Guptas (from 300 A.D. to 650 A.D.) Both Chandragupta II (375-513 A.D.) and Skandagupta (455-480 A.D.) adopted the title of Vikramāditya. The Gupta period was celebrated for a revival of Sanskrit learning and arts, and Kālidāsa is likely to have flourished in such an age. The horse sacrifice mentioned in the Mālavikāgnimitra may very well allude to the performance of that rite by Samudragupta. Kālidāsa's Prākrita also seems to belong to that period. The Kumārasambhava's title may be a covert compliment on the birth of young Kumāragupta, the son and successor of Chandragupta II. The Vikramāditya, therefore, with whom Kālidāsa is associated in tradition seems most naturally to be Chandragupta II who reigned from 380 A.D. to 413 A.D. Kālidāsa may then safely be placed about 400 A.D. A.B. Keith holds the same view.

 The opinion that Kālidāsa belongs to the Gupta period appears to be erroneous. The facts stated above in support of it are not entirely correct and further do not warrant the conclusion deduced from them. In the first place there is no basis for the view that the horse-sacrifice stated in the Mālavikāgnimitra is Samudragupta's horse-sacrifice. The play mentions Pushpamitra as the initiator of the sacrifice, and there is no justification to take it as referring to somebody else. Secondly, the statement that Kālidāsa's Prākrita belongs to the Gupta period is no more than a conjecture. Thirdly, the ingenious implication read in Kumārasambhava's title is a mere assumption. Lastly, though Kālidāsa no doubt belonged to the period of the revival of Hindu culture, it does not follow that it was the Gupta period.

 Indian tradition mentions Kālid*ā*sa as a contemporary and a court-poet of king Vikramāditya who ruled at Ujjain and founded the era after him which is accepted as commencing with 57 B.C. Some scholars once did not take this date to be correct, but brought Vikramāditya down to A.D. 544, propounding what was known as the Korur theory. According to this theory, based on Fergusson's suggestion, the era of 57 B.C. commemorated a real victory over the Hunas in A.D. 544, the reckoning being antedated 600 years. Max Muller also held the view but on
different grounds that Kālidāsa flourished about the same period. The theory has been definitely demolished by the discovery of the Mandasor Inscription which is dated Samvat 494, thereby showing conclusively that the Vikrama era existed before A.D. 544. The 1st century B.C. which the Indian tradition assigns to Kālidāsa is further supported by very cogent arguments. A terracota plaque was discovered at Bhitā, which depicts a scene from Act I of Śakuntalā. On archaeological grounds it is believed to be earlier than the Christian era. Aśvaghośa, the author of Buddhacharita, has numerous ideas and passages similar to those occurring in the works of Kālidāsa. Now Aśvaghośa was more of a philosopher than of a poet and may with greater probability be supported to have borrowed from Kālidāsa. AśvaghoŚa was a contemporary of Kanishka and lived about 78 A D. Kālidāsa, therefore, will have to be placed earlier than 78 A.D. Dr. Peterson also corroborates this view when he says, 'Kālidása stands near the beginning of the Christian era, if, indeed, he does not overtop it.'

 Internal evidence too points to the same conclusion. The references to astronomy and law of inheritance and the penalty for theft in Śakuntalā indicate a period before the Christian era. The artificiality of diction and the fondness for long compounds and *double entendre* which marks the writings of Daṇdiṇ, Bāṇa, Bhavabhūti and others who flourished about the 7th century A.D. are entirely absent in Kālidāsa's writings, This requires a period of six or seven centuries to bring about such a revolution in the style of literary composition.

 It thus seems that the Indian tradition according to which Kālidāsa lived in the 1st century is not wrong.

**3. The works of Kālidāsa.**

 The following works are indisputably acknowledged to be Kālidāsa's :—

\(i\) शकुन्तला (ii) विक्रमोर्वशीय (iii) मालविकाग्निमित्र (iv) रघुवंश (v) कुमार सम्भव (vi) मेघदूत ऋतुसंहार also is recognised to be a work of Kālidāsa, but there are some critics who contest its claim to his authorship. An effort is made to fix the sequence of the composition of these works. But this is merely conjectural and the arguments advanced are more childish than mature, more ridiculous than serious. That such an attempt is made without any data to go upon, only shows the puerile lengths to which sometimes even the most learned scholarship goes.

**4. The birth-place of Kālidāsa**

 The birth-place of Kālidāsa is as much a matter of doubt and uncertainty as is his date. The questions of his place of birth, parentage, family etc., must remain unanswered. The most widely accepted notion which has tradition for its basis is that he belonged to Benares. In view of his references to Ujjain in his poems, especially Meghadūta, which betray his love for that city, it is thought that he was born there. But the data for such an inference is too meagre. The claim has also been advanced that he was a native of Bengal. There is nothing at all to support such a wild claim. Some would fain make him an inhabitant of Kāshmir, for he happens to be the only poet who describes a saffron flower which grows in Kāshmir alone. There are others who drag him as far as Ceylon and make him a contemporary of king Kumāradāsa. This is a mere myth. In the labyrinth of these conflicting claims we must remain content with the simple assertion that Kālidāsa was born somewhere in India!

**5. Kālidāsa as a man.**

 Though the works of Kālidāsa throw no light on his life, they give us some insight into him as a man. Brāhmaṇa by birth he was a devout worshipper of Śiva but not at all a sectarian. He shows first-hand acquaintance with court-life and never hints, directly or indirectly, at being ever in straitened circumstances. His writings further reveal him to be a man possessed of a noble nature, a pure mind and a high soul. He seems to have been equally at home in the village and in the city, in the sublime solitudes of Nature and in the din and bustle of the busy haunts of life. His delineation of Nature and of Indian men and manners is vivid and accurate.

———————————————————————————————

 It is evident that he was a great traveller. He shows familiarity not only with North India but also with the South. His description of Raghu's expedition of conquest and of the journey of the cloud-messenger shows his intimate acquaintance with the geography of India.

 Unlike most poets he appears to have been very popular during his life-time. He was an admirer of hunting as a sport and describes its wholesome effects with the spirit of a sportsman. He is sometimes represented as a voluptuary. There could not be a more false and malicious estimate. Though fond of pleasures and liberal in his outlook and views, he seems to have been a man who believed in virtue and exalted it as against vice.

 He was well-versed in the scriptural lores, in grammar, in rhetoric and in philosophy. His knowledge of the Vedas, the Upanishads, the Purāṇas, the philosophical systems of Saṃkhya, Yoga and Vedānta, Medicine and the rudiments of Astronomy goes to prove that he was a widely read man. He also knew many of the truths of geography and natural science. A most cultured, accomplished and learned but modest gentleman, Kālidāsa was familiar with the graces, the refinements and the charms of human life. Ryder, like a true artist, draws a pen-picture of our poet in the following beautiful words:—

 "One feels certain that he was physically handsome and the handsome Hindu is a worderfully fine type of manhood. One knows that he possessed a fascination for women, as they in turn fascinated him. One knows that children loved him. One becomes convinced that he never suffered any morbid, soul-shaking experience such as besetting religious doubt brings with it, or the pangs of despised love; that on the contrary he moved among men and women with a serene and God-like tread, neither self-indulgent nor ascetic, with mind and senses ever alert to every form of beauty. We know that his poetry was popular while he lived, and we cannot doubt that his personality was equally attractive, though it is probable that no contemporary knew the full measure of his greatness. For his nature was one of singular balance, equally at home in a splendid court and on a lonely mountain with men of high and low degree. Such men are never fully appreciated during life. They continue to grow after they are dead."

 In a word, Kālidāsa the man is as unique as Kālidāsa the poet.

**6. The style of Kālidāsa.**

 The style of Kālidāsa is inimitable and no other poet has been able to attain his literary excellence. There is a flow, a rhythm, a music in his poetry, which affords exquisite delight to the reader and has ever remained a cherished but unattainable ideal for all aspirants to glory in the world of literature. Aśvaghosha, Vatsabhaṭṭi, Bhavabhūti, and others even of a lesser calibre have tried to imitate him but have singularly failed in their attempts. Simplicity and lucidity combined with elegance, refinement and a taste for all that is beautiful—a combination rarely found—are the marked characteristcs of his style. In short, he is the master of the Vidarbha style, 'the essentials of which are absence of long compounds, the harmony of sound, clearness, elevation and force allied to beauty such as is conveyed to language by the use of figures of speech and thought.' He rightly regards sentiment as the soul of poetry and his ornaments also serve to bring it into bold relief. Nowhere are they allowed to overwhelm it. He is conscious of the charm of the figures of speech and uses them to good effect. He is famous for his similes[^9] but is no less happy in the use of other figures of speech, especially Arthāntaranyāsa. He is, however, extremely sparing in the use of Ślesha or *double entendre*.

[^9]: # "'उपमा कालिदासस्य'।"

 He has a marvellous command over the language and is never at a loss for an appropriate word to express an idea. He uses the right word at the right place. Further, he possesses the great merit of putting into the mouths of his characters a language which they use in their everyday speech. The king, for instance, with his culture and courtesy speaks in refined and dignified Sanskrit while the policeman with his overweening and overbearing manners employs a rough and harsh dialect.

 His discriptions, especially of Nature, are vivid and graphic, and the pen-pictures which he draws of the gorgeous scenery of the Himālayas, the playful waters of the Ganges, the peaceful penance-grove with its deer and other wild animals, etc. etc., are simply superb. The effect is all the more striking when he interweaves human emotions with the beauties of Nature. It is difficult to say whether he is more of a poet of Nature or of the human heart.

 Not only does he possess a sweetness and charm of expression all his own, but he is also rich in thought and imagination. His language has also 'the merit of suggestiveness what Bhavabhūti, the greatest of his successors expresses at length, he is content to indicate by a touch.'

 He has enriched Sanskrit with pithy and pregnant sayings which abound in his writings and which have passed into proverbs and are on the lips of the Sanskrit student and scholar alike.

**7. Kālidāsa as a poet.**

 As a poet, Kalidasa has no equal. He stands head and shoulders above 'the sons of Muse'. Both ancient and modern Pandits are lavish in their praise of his genius. The following verse contains a significant tribute to the peerless position he occupies amongst the galaxy of great poets :—

**पुराकवीनां गणनाप्रसङ्गे  
 कनिष्ठिकाधिष्टितकालिदासा।  
अद्यापि तत्तुल्यकवेरभावात्  
 अनामिका सार्थवती बभूव॥**

 "Once when the poets were enumerated, Kālidāsa was counted as the first on the little finger. As he has had no peer even to this day, the next finger (अनामिका) remains true to its name."

 Bāṇa, himself a writer of great merit, thus speaks of the muse of Kālidāsa :—

**निर्गतासु न वा कस्य कालिदासस्य सूक्तिषु।  
प्रीतिर्मधुरसान्द्रासु मञ्जरीष्विव जायते॥ (Harsha-charita)**

**"Where find a soul that does not thri॥  
In Kālidāsa's verse to meet  
The smooth, inevitable lines  
Life blossom-clusters, honey-sweet ?"**

 Not only has our poet won renown and recognition in the land of his own birth, but his name and fame have also travelled far beyond the shores of India. Sir William Jones has called him 'the Shakespeare of India'. It is the highest tribute that could be paid to Kālidāsa by a foreign scholar. Prof. Lassen regards him as 'the brightest star in the firmament of Indian poetry.' Sir M. Monier Williams offers his meed of praise to the poet when he speaks of Śakuntala in the following words :—

 "No composition of Kālidāsa displays more the richness of his poetical genius, the exuberance of his imagination, the warmth and play of his fancy, his profound knowledge of the human heart, his delicate appreciation of its most refined and tender emotions, his familiarity with the workings and counter-workings of its conflicting feelings—in short more entitles him to rank as the Shakespeare of India." Humboldt says in the same strain :—

 "Kālidāsa, the celebrated author of the Śakuntala, is a masterly describer of the influence which Nature exercises upon the minds of lovers ...Tenderness in the expression of feeling and richness of creative fancy have assigned to him a lofty place among the poets of all nations."

 The well-known critic Schlegel also gives him an exalted position amongst the 'Sons of Song'. It was however left to Goethe, the greatest poet of Germany, to bestow an eulogy upon Śakuntala in lines which have made our poet immortal for all time to come :—

**"Would'st thou the Young year's blossoms and the  
fruits of its decline,**

**And all by which the soul is charmed, enraptured,  
feasted, fed,**

**Would'st thou the earth and heaven itself in one sole  
name combine ?**

**I name thee, O Śakuntala, and all at once is said."**

 In a nutshell it may be said that 'Kalidasa is India and India is Kālidāsa.' One may go even further and say with Mr. Benoy Kumar Sirkār, "Nobody understands Asia who does not understand Kālidāsa—the spirit of Asia."

**APPENDIX 11**

**THE PLAY**

**1. The Plot**

 The Mālavikāgnimitra is a Nāṭaka in five acts. It is a love-drama that is woven round a love-intrigue of king Agnimitra of Vidiśā, the hero of the play. Mālavikā, a Vidarbha princess, is the heroine. The scene is laid in Vidiśā and its vicinity.

 King Agnimitra happens to see Mālavikā in a picture, and is fascinated by her beauty. Mālavikā has been sent to Dhāriṇī, the chief queen of Agnimitra, by her brother Vīrasena as a present and is placed by her in charge of Gaṇadāsa to learn dancing. The king asks Vidūshaka to help him to obtain a sight of Mālavikā, as Dhāriṇī is trying to keep her out of his way. Vidūshaka provokes a quarrel between Gaṇadāsa and Hardatta, the two masters of the dance patronized by the queen and the king respectively. They both approach the king for decision as to which of them is superior in the art of dancing. The king invites in this connection the opinion of Dhāriṇi and Kauśikī, a female ascetic who has recently joined the queen's entourage. It is decided, not-withstanding the queen's wishes to the contrary, that the two masters of dancing should each stage a dance by his best pupil before all and Kauśikī should be the judge. (Act 1)

 Gaṇadāsa is given the first chance ann accordingly Mālavikā appears on the stage. Her singing and dancing delight all. The king is all the more charmed by her beauty and falls head over ears in love with her. The object of the king to have a sight of Mālavikā being achieved, the exhibition of Haradatta's pupil is postponed to another date on the score that the dinner-time has arrived. (Act II)

 The interlude in which two maid-servants are shown in conversation reveals that Gaṇadāsa has won in the contest, that the king is love-lorn and that Mālavikā is now being kept out of the king's sight still more assiduously. The scene changes to the royal garden where Dhāriṇī has deputed Mālavikā to fulfil the longing of the Aśoka tree by kicking it with her foot with the promise of a substantial reward if it blossoms within five nights of the kick. The queen herself cannot perform the ceremony, as her foot has been injured by a fall from the swing as a result of Vidūshaka's mischief. Bakulāvalikā, a maid-servant of the queen, who is won over to the king's cause by Vidūshaka, while painting Mālavikā's foot in the royal garden, draws out by her clever talk Mālavikā to the extent that she reveals her love for the king. The king also has come to the garden for a swing at the bid-ding of Irāvatī, his younger and favourite queen. The king with the Vidūshaka and Irávatī with her maid-servant, Nipuṇika, unseen by each other, overhear Mālavikā and Bakulāvalikā from whom they are hidden. The king on finding that his love is reciprocated enters and wants to embrace Mālavikā. Irāvati starles them by her sudden appearance on the scene. Mālavikā and Bakulāvalikā go of in fright, Irāvatī, who is suspicious and jealous of any rival in the king's love, insults him and leaves him in rage despite his entreaties. (ACT III)

 Dhāriṇi, on being informed of the incident by Irāvatī, has both Mālavikā and Bakulāvalikā put in fetters in an underground cell with her maid-servant mounting guard and instructed not to release them unless the queen's signet-ring bearing the figure of a snake is shown to her. Vidūshaka pretends having been bitten by a snake and appears in that condition before Dhāriṇī at the place where she is resting with her injured foot being held by her attendant and with the king and Kauśikī keeping her company. The Vidūshaka is sent for treatment to the court-physician, Dhruvasiddhi, who is also a poison-specialist. The court-physician being in the confidence of Vidūshaka sends word that he wants something bearing the figure of a snake for curing him. The unsuspecting Dhāriṇī is taken in by the ruse and hands over her signet-ring which is employed for the more useful end of securing the release of Mālavikā and Bakulāvalikā. Vidūshaka now leaves both of them in the summer-house to wait there for the king whom he hastens to inform of the success of the plan. The lovers meet, but are again surprised by Irāvatī whose vigilance has not abated. The king finds himself in a quandary from which he is saved by the timely announcement of the news of an accident to the little princess Vasulakshmi at the hands of a monkey. All leave in a hurry. Mālavikā and Bakulāvalikā also depart, alternating with fear and hope—fear because of Irāvatī and hope because of the blossoming of the Aśoka and the consequent reward promised by Dhāriṇī. (Act IV)

 Two unexpected pieces of news bring matters to a climax. The victory of the forces sent against the king of Vidarbha is announced. Two maids sent as presents from Vidarbha appear in the court at Vidiśā and recognize both Mālavikā, their princess and the sister of prince Mādhavasena, and Kauśikī, the sister of Mādhavasena's noble minister Sumati. Kauśikī, who has kept silent over the identity of Mālavikā in obedience to a prophecy by a sage that Mālavikā would remain in servitude for one year before she is united with a suitable husband, now relates how on prince Mādhavasena being captured by his cousin Yajñasena her brother Sumati escaped with Mālavikā and herself, how they were waylaid in the forest by a gang of bandits, how Sumati paid with his dear life the debt of affection to his masters, how she was

separated from Mālavikā how she came to Vidiṣā and put on the robes of an ascetic, and how later on Mālavikā also arrived there as a present from Vīrasena, Dhārini's brother. Further Pushpamitra, Agnimitra's father, sends tidings of victory in the north. Vasumitra, the son of Dhāriṇi, who was placed in charge of the sacrificial horse let loose by Pushpamitra, who wanted to perform the horse-sacrifice, has defeated the Yavanas on the bank of the Sindhu when the horse was seized by them. The king orders that the kingdom of Vidarbha should be divided equally between the two cousins, Yajṇasena and Mādhavasena. Dhāriṇi, who already has given Mālavikā a word that she would reward her on the blossoming of Aśoka in time, is delighted at the news of her son's victory and offers the hand of Mālavikā to the king. He accepts her as his wife. Irāvatī also bows before the inevitable and begs her pardon of the king. Thus there is a happy *denouement. *(Act V).

**2. The authorship of the play.**

 Some western scholars doubted that Kālidāsa was the author of Mālavikāgnimitra. Prof. H. H. Wilson thought that the play might have been written by another Kālidāsa. He says that 'there is neither the same melody in the verse nor fancy in the thoughts as in the Vikramorvaśīya and Śakuntala, and that "the manners described appear to be that of a degenerate state of Hindu Society." The question was fully discussed by the late Mr.Shankar Pandurang Pandit in his preface to the edition of the play and it was established by him beyond all doubt that the author of Mālavikāgnimitra was *the same* Kālidāsa who wrote the Śakuntala, and not *some other* Kālidāsa as assumed by Wilson. The controversy ended with Mr.Pandit's reply and now Mālavikāgnimitra is universally acknowledged to be the work of Kālidasā, the author of Śakuntala. It is fatuous to say that the melody in the Mālavikagnimitra is less than in the two sister-plays. It is equally wrong to maintain that there is little of fancy in the thoughts of Mālavikāgnimitra. The charge of degenerate manners of society is, as Mr.Pandit puts it. 'little deserved' by Mālavikāgnimitra. The manners are the same as depicted in the other two plays. If these are degenerate, they are so in all the three plays. Agnimitra is as pure and chivalrous as Dushyanta and Puṛuravas. Polygamy is permitted to a Hindu even now and is the general rule among Hindu princes. If it were to be said that Agnimitra was already married, the answer is that Dushyanta and Pururavas were already married as well.

 Moreover, Indian opinion based on literary tradition is unanimous in holding Mālavikāgnimitra to be a production of Kālidāsa. Both external and internal evidence prove that all the three plays are the work of the same hand. The peculiar style of Kālidāsa is there in all of them—the same simplicity, the same charm of expression, the same words, phrases and ideas. All this unmistakably points out Kālidāsa's authorship of the play and there is no doubt that Kālidāsa, the author of Śakuntala and Vikramôrvaśīya, is the author of Mālavikāgnimitra also.

**3. The historical background of the play.**
